item_1.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-828]. The client is Brenda Lang, and the matter is Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet.

In a series of email communications regarding the case, dated between October 24 and October 29, 2025, attorney-to-attorney discussions took place about a potential settlement offer. The principal participants were Aaron Whaley of Whaley Law Firm and Scott Gowen, with Brenda D. Long copied on the exchanges.

On October 24, 2025, Aaron Whaley sent an email acknowledging that Scott Gowen had been representing Marcus Hamlet and had been dealing with Bryce Koon, who was Aaron’s former associate. Aaron stated that Bryce is no longer his associate, and he understood that last he and Bryce had left off with a $15,000 offer to resolve the claim, asking Scott to confirm whether that reflected the current position. The message concluded with thanks.

By October 28, 2025, Aaron sent another message at 12:32 PM to Scott Gowen and Brenda D. Long, enclosing the previous point for clarity. The subject was Re: Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet. In this communication, Aaron included a caution note: “CAUTION: This email came from outside of Louisville Metro. Do not click links, open attachments, or give away private information unless you recognize the sender’s email address and know the content is safe.” He wrote, “Hey Scott, just circling back on this. Thanks!”

Scott Gowen replied around 12:40 PM on October 28, 2025. His response acknowledged a late reply and stated that he did not have authority on the matter but had told Bryce that he would be willing to recommend $15,000 if Ms. Lang would agree to take it. He concluded with thanks.

On October 29, 2025, at 01:21:43 GMT-04:00, Aaron Whaley sent a final note to Scott Gowen stating, “Scott, we will take $15,000 to get this done.” This message was sent from Aaron Whaley of Whaley Law Firm, with Brenda D. Long cc’d on the email.

The email exchanges also included standard confidentiality language typical of firm communications: “The information contained in this communication from the sender is confidential. It is intended solely for use by the recipient and others authorized to receive it. If you are not the recipient, you are hereby notified that any disclosure, copying, distribution or taking action in relation to the contents of this information is strictly prohibited and may be unlawful.” The messages concluded with Aaron Whaley’s signature, including Whaley Law Firm’s contact information (712 Lyndon Ln, Louisville, KY 40222; phone [PHONE-7]).

item_5.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-23]. Client: Brenda Lang. This matter is Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet, Jefferson County case 23-CI-005931, involving a motor vehicle accident originally noted on March 9, 2021 (Brenda Lang MVA). The following is a narrative of the communications and settlement/discovery discussions among counsel from The Whaley Law Firm and related parties, centered on potential settlement with Metro Government and depositions of various individuals.

The chronology begins with March 26, 2025, when Scott Gowen of Metro Government suggested that it might make sense to set a trial date and “work this one up,” signaling openness to moving toward resolution but not yet committing to any particular figure or release terms. On April 29, 2025, Bryce Koon of The Whaley Law Firm replied that, after reviewing the file, the parties would likely need to depose the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle and asked for potential dates for those individuals.

On May 5, 2025, Bryce again pressed for depositions, stating that Lang’s side intended to depose Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight, and Scott Gowen responded later that morning, requesting that Bryce identify exactly who they wished to depose so he could verify whether those individuals were still employed by Metro Government. A week later, on May 21, 2025, Bryce followed up for an update. On May 27, 2025, Gowen replied that he was still verifying the employment status of the individuals and expected to have information within a day or two.

By June 30, 2025, Bryce indicated that Lang’s side was prepared to reduce its demand to $30,000 for a full and final settlement of the claim, and he asked again which individuals could be deposed, seeking potential dates for those depositions, and noting that the other passengers in Lang’s vehicle would need to be deposed as well.

In July 2025, discussions shifted to a potential settlement with Metro Government. On July 21, 2025, Bryce sent a message acknowledging the ongoing discussions and seeking updates. On July 23, 2025, Gowen replied, stating that while he did not currently have authority, he would be willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $15,000, provided there was a release containing broad indemnity language and only if Ms. Lang would accept that amount. He asked Bryce to confirm whether Lang was interested in that proposition. Bryce then responded on July 24, 2025, at 10:42 AM, asking for thoughts and expressing doubt that Lang would agree to a $15,000 settlement. Later the same day, at 11:41 AM, Aaron Whaley, counsel for Lang, weighed in on the matter by asking whether the appendix to the medical records should be excluded from consideration and, if the appendix is excluded, what the total medicals would be; he also sought Lang’s confirmation that she considers the appendix to be unrelated.

Throughout this sequence, the messages include the standard outside-email caution notices about the sender’s address and privilege, with boilerplate confidentiality language affirming that the communications are intended for the named recipients and advising on handling if received in error. The parties’ communications reflect a pattern of ongoing discovery planning (depositions of named individuals and of other passengers in Lang’s vehicle) alongside ongoing settlement discussions with Metro Government, including a potential settlement at $30,000, then a later proposal of $15,000 conditioned on a broad indemnity release and Lang’s acceptance. The case remains active with the plaintiff’s representation by The Whaley Law Firm and involvement from Metro Government counsel.

item_6.note: Between March 2025 and July 2025, counsel in Brenda Lang’s motor vehicle collision case with Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson County, case 23-CI-005931) exchanged a series of email updates. The case is identified in these notes as Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson 23-CI-005931), with Brenda Lang as the client. The communications below involve W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, and Scott Gowen of Louisville Metro Government, with several emails including a Filevine project CC for Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-809]. Each message carried the standard attorney‑client privilege disclaimer stating the message is intended only for the named recipient and should not be forwarded if received in error, and, in several instances, a caution that the email originated from outside Louisville Metro.

The first entry (July 21, 2025) shows Bryce Koon following up on the case and asking Scott Gowen for any update. The message is brief: “Following up on this. Any update appreciated.” It includes Bryce’s signature and the firm’s contact details, along with the customary privilege disclaimer, and a separate note indicating the message is intended only for the named recipient and should not be used or forwarded if received in error. The email also includes a Filevine CC line referencing Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-808].

Earlier, on June 30, 2025, Bryce wrote to counsel with a substantive update: they were following up and asking whether certain individuals were available, and in the meantime, they were prepared to reduce their demand to $30,000 for a full and final settlement of the claim. This message also carries the standard privilege disclaimer and the outside-email caution in the body of the message.

On May 27, 2025, Scott Gowen replied that he was still verifying that each of the named individuals were still employed by Metro Government and that he should know something today or tomorrow. This message also included the privilege disclaimer and the outside-email caution.

The May 21, 2025 correspondence from Bryce Koon to Gowen (with Brenda Lang’s Filevine CC) is a follow-up note that continues the thread of inquiry, and again includes the “CAUTION: This email came from outside of Louisville Metro” notice. It carries the same privilege disclaimer as the other messages.

Earlier still, on May 5, 2025, Bryce wrote that they intended to depose Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight. Scott Gowen’s response that day asked Bryce to identify exactly who he wanted to depose so he could check whether those individuals remained employed by Metro Government. The reply from Bryce confirms the intended deponents and maintains the same formal email structure and privilege notice.

On May 5, 2025, in a prior exchange, Gowen instructed Bryce to specify precisely who should be deposed and indicated he would verify employment status. Bryce then indicated the specific deponents he wished to depose.

On April 29, 2025, Bryce advised that after reviewing the file, it appeared depositions of the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle would be necessary, and he requested potential dates for those individuals. The message also included the standard privilege disclaimer and the outside-email caution.

On March 26, 2025, Gowen suggested that it might make sense to set a trial date and work the case up from there, signaling a shift toward trial planning.

On March 25, 2025, Bryce responded to Gowen, expressing confusion about a disagreement on the relatedness of medical records. He explained that he had removed any “questionably-related” treatment regarding Ms. Lang’s appendix and asserted that the remaining medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. He asked whether the 9/12/21 emergency room treatment was not related to the collision and requested that Gowen explain if that was the case; he offered to resend the records if needed. The March 25 message also carried the usual privilege disclaimer and a caution about outside email origin.

Across these messages, the topic centers on discovery planning (depositions of certain witnesses, including other passengers in the vehicle), a potential settlement posture (the June 30, 2025 update reduces the demand to $30,000 for full and final settlement), and ongoing disputes over the relationship of medical records to the collision, with a thread of confirming which witnesses remain employed by Metro Government. The emails repeatedly note that the content is confidential and privileged, warn recipients about outside-origin emails, and include standard firm contact information and Filevine project references.

item_7.note: In the Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet matter (Jefferson 23-CI-005931), Brenda Lang is the client in a motor vehicle accident case designated as Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-28]. The following narrative summarizes the email correspondence between The Whaley Law Firm and Scott Gowen, counsel for Louisville Metro Government, from early 2025 through June 30, 2025, including the ongoing deposition and settlement discussions.

On March 7, 2025, Scott Gowen replied to Bryce Koon of The Whaley Law Firm. Gowen stated that he could not reach a settlement of $35,000 and that any settlement would have to include indemnity for the medicals, most of which he argued were not attributable to the accident. He indicated he had no authority to authorize a higher amount but suggested Metro Government might pay $10,000 subject to a release containing broad indemnity language, provided Brenda Lang would accept that amount; otherwise, he suggested we proceed toward a trial date.

A week later, on March 25, 2025, Bryce Koon responded. He explained that, after reviewing the file, it appeared the depositions of the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle would be required. He asked Scott Gowen to provide potential dates for those individuals. He also noted that he had previously removed “any of the ‘questionably-related’ treatment” regarding Lang’s appendix, and that the remaining medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. He asked whether the 9/12/21 emergency room treatment was considered unrelated to the collision and requested an explanation if that were the case; he offered to resend records if needed.

On March 26, 2025, Gowen replied that it might make sense to obtain a trial date and to work the case up toward that end.

In a message dated April 29, 2025, Bryce Koon wrote that, after reviewing the file, it appeared the parties would need to depose the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle, and he asked Scott Gowen for potential deposition dates for those individuals.

On May 5, 2025, Bryce indicated in a message that the team was looking to depose Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight. Gowen responded by asking Bryce to identify exactly who he wished to depose and said he would check to confirm whether those individuals were still employed by Metro Government.

Also on May 5, 2025, the messages included standard attorney-client privilege disclaimers: Bryce’s message, and Gowen’s response, each noted that the electronic communications were intended only for the named recipient and may contain privileged information, with instructions to contact The Whaley Law Firm if received in error and not to disclose or forward content to unintended recipients.

On May 21, 2025, Bryce sent a follow-up email titled Re: Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson 23-CI-005931). The email carried a caution notice indicating it originated outside Louisville Metro and reiterated the privilege disclaimer language commonly included in such communications. The body simply stated, “Good morning counsel, Just wanted to follow up on this. Thanks,” with Bryce’s contact information and firm details.

Two days later, on May 27, 2025, Scott Gowen replied that he was still verifying whether the proposed deponents—presumably Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight—remained employed by Metro Government, and he anticipated having an answer today or tomorrow.

Finally, on June 30, 2025, Bryce Koon sent a follow-up that stated, “Counsel, Wanted to follow up on this and if any of these individuals are available. In the meantime, we are prepared to reduce our demand to $30,000 for full and final settlement of this claim.” The email was sent from Bryce Koon of The Whaley Law Firm, located at 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222, with the firm’s phone and fax numbers listed, and it was addressed to Scott Gowen with a copy to the Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-29] Filevine project email address. The message included the standard protective disclaimer about privileged information and instructed that, if received in error, the recipient should contact The Whaley Law Firm and should not use or forward the information.

In summary, the case overview shows ongoing deposition planning (including Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, Eric Knight, and other passengers) and a shift in settlement posture from a prior target in the high-30s to an expressed willingness to settle for $30,000, contingent upon the availability of the named individuals and the terms of a full and final release with indemnity provisions.

item_8.note: The Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet case (Jefferson 23-CI-005931) involves client Brenda Lang, with the case file titled Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-803]. The communications in the record show a sequence of emails between W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, and Scott Gowen of Louisville Metro, with Brenda Lang’s Filevine project email address copied on certain messages. The latest entry is a follow-up from May 21, 2025.

The first dated entry in the thread occurs on February 17, 2025, when Bryce Koon wrote to counsel, “Good morning counsel, Wanted to follow up on this one. Any updated appreciated.” The message includes the standard confidentiality disclaimer common to The Whaley Law Firm emails, noting that the electronic message is intended only for the named recipient and may contain attorney‑client privileged information; recipients who receive it in error should contact the firm and should not use or forward the information. A caution that the message originated outside Louisville Metro is also included in the record.

On March 5, 2025, Bryce Koon sent another message: “Good afternoon counsel, Just circling back on this one again.” This message, too, carried the outside-LouVolle Metro caution and the same confidentiality disclaimer.

On March 7, 2025, Scott Gowen replied: “Bryce, Sorry for the delay in responding. I cannot get to $35K. And any settlement would have to include indemnity for the medicals, most of which are not even attributable to the accident. I do not have any authority, but would be willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $10K subject to the execution of a release containing broad indemnity language. But I am only willing to make that recommendation if you tell me that Ms. Lang will accept that amount. Otherwise, I guess we just need to get a trial date.” This message also includes the standard confidentiality disclaimer and the outside‑the‑Metro caution.

On March 25, 2025, Bryce Koon responded: “I'm sorry Scott, but I guess I am missing something here as we are not on the same page. I have removed any of the ‘questionably-related’ treatment regarding Ms. Lang's appendix. The remainder of the medicals, as identified in the demand, are clearly related to the collision. Is it your position that the 9/12/21 ER treatment was not related to the collision? If that is the case, please explain. If you need me to resend these records, please let me know.” This message again carries the outside‑Louisville caution and the standard disclaimer.

On March 26, 2025, Gowen replied: “Bryce, I understand your position. Perhaps it makes sense to get a trial date and we can work this one up.” The message includes the usual confidentiality notice and outside‑Metro caution.

Then, on April 29, 2025, Bryce Koon wrote: “Good afternoon counsel, After reviewing this file, it appears we will need to take the depositions of the other passengers in your client's vehicle. Are you able to provide potential dates for any of those individuals?” This email had the external-caution disclaimer and the confidentiality notice, and it CC’ed Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-802] - Filevine Project Email Address, as well as Sarena Tuttle.

Next, on May 5, 2025, there were two messages. The first, at about 9:15 AM, was from Scott Gowen: “Bryce, Sorry for the late response. Please identify exactly who you want to take and I will check to see if they are still employed by Metro Government.” The message again contained the customary confidentiality disclaimer and the outside‑Louisville caution. A few minutes later, Bryce Koon replied: “Good morning counsel, We are looking to depose Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight.” This reply also included the same disclaimers and caution.

Finally, on May 21, 2025, Bryce Koon sent another follow-up to counsel: “Good morning counsel, Just wanted to follow up on this. Thanks,” signed with his name and title from The Whaley Law Firm, The message again carried the confidentiality disclaimer and the standard outside‑Louisville Metro caution.

Across these exchanges, the record reflects a focus on scheduling depositions (specifically of Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, Eric Knight, and the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle) and ongoing negotiations regarding settlement amounts and indemnity for medicals, as well as consideration of trial timing. The thread includes multiple CCs to Brenda Lang’s Filevine address and notes the involvement of Sarena Tuttle in some communications. Each email contains a confidential attorney‑client disclaimer and a caution about outside communication.

item_9.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-35]
Client: Brenda Lang

Re: Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson 23-CI-005931)

This matter concerns Brenda Lang and her automobile-accident case against Marcus Hamlet, docketed in Jefferson County as 23-CI-005931. The following narrative summarizes the email communications among counsel and the parties from February through May 2025.

On February 17, 2025, W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, sent a message to counsel regarding the Lang v. Hamlet matter, stating, “Good morning counsel, Wanted to follow up on this one. Any updated appreciated.” The message included Koon’s contact details and a standard attorney-client privilege disclaimer, noting that the electronic message is intended only for the named recipient and warning against disclosure if received in error.

On March 5, 2025, at 12:20 PM, Bryce Koon again wrote to Scott Gowen with Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-36] – Filevine Project Email Address cc’d to Sarena Tuttle. In this outside-Louville Metro email, Koon said, “Good afternoon counsel, Just circling back on this one again,” and signed with his firm’s contact information. A CAUTION notice warned that the email originated outside Louisville Metro.

On March 7, 2025, at 9:22 AM, Gowen replied, telling Bryce that he could not reach $35,000 in settlement and that any settlement would need to include indemnity for medicals, the majority of which, he claimed, were not attributable to the accident. Gowen stated he did not have authority but was willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $10,000 subject to the execution of a release with broad indemnity language, provided Lang would accept that amount; otherwise, the parties would proceed toward a trial date. This reply also included a standard confidentiality disclaimer.

On March 25, 2025, at 1:29 PM, Bryce Koon responded to Gowen, again including Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-797] – Filevine Project Email Address and Sarena Tuttle in CC. He expressed that he believed they were not on the same page, noting that he had removed “any of the ‘questionably-related’ treatment regarding Ms. Lang’s appendix” and that the remaining medicals, as identified in the demand, were clearly related to the collision. He asked Gowen to explain if the 9/12/21 ER treatment was not related to the collision and offered to resend records if needed. The message carried the standard outside-email CAUTION.

On March 26, 2025, at 10:56 AM, Gowen replied to Bryce, acknowledging Bryce’s position and suggesting that it might make sense to obtain a trial date and develop the case from there. The message concluded with Gowen’s sign-off, followed by the typical disclaimer about privileged or protected information.

On April 29, 2025, at 2:18 PM, Bryce Koon wrote to Gowen with Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-796] – Filevine Project Email Address and Sarena Tuttle copied in. He reported that, after reviewing the file, it appeared they would need to depose the other passengers in Lang’s vehicle and asked Gowen to provide potential dates for those individuals. The message included the standard outside-email caution.

On May 5, 2025, there were two key exchanges. At 9:15 AM, Gowen replied to Bryce, apologizing for the late response and asking Bryce to identify exactly who he wanted to depose so he could check whether those individuals were still employed by Metro Government. Bryce’s response requested the depositions of Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight. This May 5 message from Bryce stated the deposition targets explicitly and was accompanied by his firm’s signature block, including address, phone, and fax, as well as a confidentiality disclaimer.

The May 5 exchange also reflects the ongoing discovery posture in the case, including a focus on depositions of individuals connected to Lang Lang v. Hamlet, with the primary objective of setting depositions for the potential witnesses named by Bryce Koon, Esq.

In summary, the thread from February through May 2025 shows ongoing settlement negotiations centered on indemnity for medicals and a dispute over the appropriate settlement amount, a proposal to move forward with a trial date, and a clear discovery plan that included deposing the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle and, later, specifically deposing Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight, as the case progressed toward trial. The correspondence repeatedly involves The Whaley Law Firm (Bryce Koon, Esq.), Scott Gowen (Metro Government counsel), Brenda Lang (the plaintiff), and Sarena Tuttle, with standard confidentiality cautions included in the outgoing emails.

item_10.note: In the Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet matter (Jefferson 23-CI-005931), the case team carried on a series of settlement and discovery discussions from January through May of 2025. The proceedings centered on settlement posture, the scope of medical-related demands, and plans to depose potential witnesses and other passengers involved in the collision.

On January 27, 2025, Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, sent a revised settlement demand regarding the above-referenced matter. He noted that all references to appendix-related issues had been removed and that the remainder of the demand addressed the issues identified in the demand by treating the medicals as related to the collision. The email included the standard attorney-client privilege disclaimer, and the message indicated the revised demand was attached. The set of recipients included Scott Gowen from Louisville Metro Government, with copies to Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-40] - Filevine Project Email Address and Sarena Tuttle, among others.

On February 17, 2025, Bryce Koon followed up, asking for any updates on the matter. No substantive changes to positions were recorded in the note, but the outreach reflected ongoing interest in progress.

By March 5, 2025, Koon again contacted Gowen, stating that he believed the two sides remained out of alignment. He explained that he had removed any “questionably-related” treatment regarding Ms. Lang’s appendix, and he asserted that the remaining medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. He asked Gowen to indicate if the 9/12/21 ER treatment was not related to the collision and offered to resend the records if needed. The message carried the external-email caution typical of communications from outside Louisville Metro.

On March 7, 2025, Gowen replied that he could not reach a $35,000 settlement and that any settlement would need to include indemnity for the medicals, most of which he contended were not attributable to the accident. He stated he did not have authority to approve a settlement, but was willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $10,000, subject to the execution of a release with broad indemnity language. He emphasized that this recommendation depended on Ms. Lang’s acceptance, and otherwise suggested proceeding to trial.

The exchange continued on March 25, 2025, with Koon responding that he believed there remained a misunderstanding. He stated that he had removed the “questionably-related” appendix issues and that the remainder of the medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. He again asked Gowen to explain whether the 9/12/21 ER treatment was not related to the collision, offering to resend the records if needed.

Gowen followed up on March 26, 2025, suggesting that it might make sense to set a trial date and proceed with preparing the case toward trial rather than continuing the current settlement discussion.

On April 29, 2025, Koon wrote that, after reviewing the file, they would need to depose the other passengers in Ms. Lang’s vehicle and asked Gowen to provide potential dates for those depositions.

Finally, on May 5, 2025, Bryce Koon sent a message to Gowen stating, “We are looking to depose Wendel Smith, Marcus Cheaton, and Eric Knight.” Gowen replied later that morning, asking Koon to identify exactly who he wanted to depose and to confirm that those individuals were still employed by Metro Government. The May 5 emails continued to carry the same privilege notices and recipient lists found in the prior messages, with copies to Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-41] - Filevine Project Email Address and Sarena Tuttle.

Across the chain, the notices reflect the ongoing effort to coordinate depositions of specific individuals (the initial three named in the May 5 email and, subsequently, the other passengers) while simultaneously negotiating a potential settlement that balances indemnity language and the attribution of medical expenses. The signature blocks consistently show The Whaley Law Firm’s address at 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222, with the standard attorney contact information, and the messages include customary cautions about outside emails and privileged information.

item_12.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-46]. Client: Brenda Lang.

Re: Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson 23-CI-005931).

This matter involves Brenda Lang and Marcus Hamlet in Jefferson County, case number 23-CI-005931. The following is a narrative of the email communications about the case, beginning with the latest message and then detailing the preceding exchanges.

On April 29, 2025, at 02:18:09 GMT-04:00, W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, sent an email to Scott Gowen, with copies to Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-47] - Filevine Project Email Address and Sarena Tuttle. The subject was the Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet matter. In the message, Koon stated that after reviewing the file, it appeared depositions of the other passengers in Brenda Lang’s vehicle would need to be taken. He asked Gowen to provide potential dates for those individuals and included his firm’s contact information: The Whaley Law Firm, 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222; phone [PHONE-786]; fax [PHONE-49]. The email carried a standard attorney-client privilege disclaimer indicating it was intended only for the named recipient and warning that if received in error, the recipient should contact The Whaley Law Firm and should not use or forward the information.

Earlier communications in the thread unfolded as follows:

On March 26, 2025, at 10:56 AM (roughly), Scott Gowen replied to Bryce Koon, indicating that he understood Koon’s position and suggesting that it might make sense to set a trial date and “work this one up.”

On March 25, 2025, at 1:29 PM, Bryce Koon wrote back to Gowen. In this message, which included a caution note stating it originated from outside Louisville Metro, Koon expressed that he believed they were not on the same page. He had removed any “questionably-related” treatment concerning Ms. Lang’s appendix from the records, maintaining that the remaining medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. He asked whether Gowen’s position was that the 9/12/21 ER treatment was not related to the collision and requested an explanation if that was the case; he offered to resend the records if needed. The message was signed with Bryce Koon’s contact information.

On March 7, 2025, at 9:22 AM, Gowen replied to Bryce Koon. He stated that he could not reach $35,000 and that any settlement would need to include indemnity for the medicals, most of which he believed were not attributable to the accident. He said he had no authority but would be willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $10,000, subject to a release containing broad indemnity language. He noted that he would only make that recommendation if Koon could confirm that Ms. Lang would accept that amount; otherwise, he suggested they should proceed to trial.

On March 5, 2025, at 12:20 PM, Bryce Koon sent a reply to Gowen (with Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-784] - Filevine Project Email Address copied). The message, which included the standard external-email caution, began with “Good afternoon counsel” and stated that he was circling back on the matter again. It concluded with a brief sign-off containing his contact details.

On February 17, 2025, at 10:23 AM, Bryce Koon sent another message to Gowen. He wrote, “Good morning counsel, Wanted to follow up on this one. Any updated appreciated. Thank you,” and again provided his contact information.

On January 27, 2025, at 12:17 PM, Bryce Koon sent a revised settlement demand to Gowen. He explained that all references to appendix-related issues and treatment had been removed, while acknowledging there were still valid questions about the connection of those issues to the collision. He recognized the difficulty of litigating such matters but described the removal as a good-faith effort to facilitate a resolution. He noted that, even with those items removed, Ms. Lang had incurred more than $26,000 in medical expenses directly related to the injuries caused by Mr. Hamlet’s negligence. He stated that an offer in the range of $5,000–$10,000 would not be sufficient to resolve the matter and expressed willingness to discuss a reasonable resolution based on documented medical expenses and Ms. Lang’s pain and suffering. He asked Gowen to let them know Metro Government’s position after reviewing the revised demand and supporting materials. The message included a Dropbox link: https://www.dropbox.com/t/ZMw14HaRDOYwjiY9. The email concluded with Bryce Koon’s signature and firm contact information, along with the standard disclaimer about attorney-client privilege and external email.

The April 29, 2025 message, referenced at the start of this narrative, was sent in the context of ongoing negotiations, with a focus on obtaining dates for depositions of other passengers and continuing to manage the litigation strategy in Brenda Lang’s favor. The thread shows a persistent effort to settle through indemnity-based releases and to address the admissibility and relevance of medical expenses tied to the collision, while exploring trial options if a settlement could not be achieved. The correspondence also reflects routine preservation of privilege notices and external-email cautions across the communications.

item_13.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-52]
Client: Brenda Lang

 Brenda Lang, the client in the matter Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson 23-CI-005931), which stems from a motor vehicle accident on September 3, 2021, has been engaging in settlement discussions with counsel for Marcus Hamlet. The communications span January through March 2025 and involve the Whaley Law Firm, led by W. Bryce Koon, Esq., and counsel for the opposing side, Scott Gowen.

 In a January 27, 2025 email, Bryce Koon sent a revised settlement demand to Scott Gowen, noting that he had removed all references to appendix-related issues and treatment. He acknowledged that while there were still questions about the connection of those appendix-related items to the collision, he understood the difficulty of litigating those points. Bryce emphasized that Ms. Lang had incurred more than $26,000 in medical expenses directly related to the injuries from the collision, and stated that an offer in the range of $5,000 to $10,000 would not be sufficient to resolve the matter. He attached supporting materials, including a Dropbox link, and provided his contact details: The Whaley Law Firm, 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222, phone [PHONE-53], fax [PHONE-54]. The email also included the standard privilege disclaimer and noted that the message was intended only for the named recipient.

 On February 17, 2025, Bryce followed up again, requesting any updated position after reviewing the revised demand and supporting materials. The message, like the January 27 email, carried the outside-source caution and the same contact information for The Whaley Law Firm.

 On March 5, 2025 at 12:20 PM, Bryce sent another follow-up to Scott Gowen, again seeking guidance on the case status. The message reiterated the firm’s earlier position and asked for any updates.

 On March 7, 2025 at 9:22 AM, Scott Gowen replied. He stated that he could not reach $35,000 in a settlement and that any settlement would have to include indemnity for the medicals, most of which he believed were not even attributable to the accident. He added that he did not have formal authority but would be willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $10,000, subject to the execution of a release containing broad indemnity language. Gowen noted, however, that he would only make that recommendation if Ms. Lang would accept that amount; otherwise, he suggested proceeding to trial.

 That same March 7 exchange was copied to Brenda Lang’s Filevine project email address and to Sarena Tuttle at the Whaley Law Firm.

 On March 25, 2025, Bryce responded with a direct rebuke of the current stance, explaining that the parties were not on the same page. He stated that he had removed all references to the appendix-related treatment from the demand and contended that the remaining medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. Bryce asked Scott whether the 9/12/21 emergency room treatment was considered unrelated to the collision and requested clarification. He also offered to resend any records if needed.

 Finally, on March 26, 2025 at 14:55:52 UTC, Scott Gowen closed the loop with a concise note to Bryce: he understood Bryce’s position and suggested that it might be prudent to obtain a trial date so the matter could be progressed.

 In summary, the case has involved a long-running settlement dialogue centered on medical expenses (Ms. Lang’s medical bills exceed $26,000 and potentially align with the collision’s injuries), contested connections of certain treatment and appendix-related issues, and discussions about a potential, limited indemnity-driven settlement from Metro Government (around $10,000) versus proceeding to trial, with an initial position rejecting a $35,000 figure. The communications were shared among counsel for Ms. Lang (The Whaley Law Firm), counsel for Marcus Hamlet (Scott Gowen of the Metro Government), and Ms. Lang herself via the Filevine project, with copies to Sarena Tuttle.

item_14.note: In the Brenda Lang motor-vehicle accident matter (Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-56]), Brenda Lang is the client, and the case is Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Jefferson 23-CI-005931). Beginning with a series of email exchanges in early 2025, the parties discussed settlement prospects and the scope of medicals tied to the collision.

On January 27, 2025, W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, sent a revised settlement demand to counsel for Marcus Hamlet. In that message, Koon stated that all references to appendix-related treatment and issues had been removed. He explained that, although there were still questions about the connection of those appendix-related items to the collision, the removal was meant to demonstrate good faith to facilitate resolution. He noted that Ms. Lang had incurred more than $26,000 in medical expenses directly related to the injuries caused by the negligence of Hamlet, and that an offer in the range of $5,000 to $10,000 would be insufficient to resolve the matter. Koon expressed willingness to discuss a reasonable resolution based on the documented medical expenses and Ms. Lang’s pain and suffering and asked the recipient to review the revised demand and supporting materials, including a Dropbox link. The message carried the standard confidentiality notice common to attorney communications and was signed with full contact details for The Whaley Law Firm. The email also referenced that the CC line included Brenda Lang MVA [DOB-777]’s Filevine project email address and Sarena Tuttle.

On February 17, 2025, Bryce Koon followed up with counsel, writing, “Good morning counsel, Wanted to follow up on this one again. Any updated appreciated,” reiterating the need for a response. This message also carried the firm’s standard confidentiality notice.

On March 5, 2025, another outside-email cautionary notice preceded Koon’s message, in which he again wrote, “Good afternoon counsel, Just circling back on this one again.” He included his contact details and the firm’s confidentiality notice.

On March 7, 2025, Scott Gowen replied. He stated that he could not get to a settlement figure of $35,000 and that any settlement would need to include indemnity for the medicals, noting that most of the medical expenses were not even attributable to the accident. He indicated that he did not have authority to settle but that he would be willing to recommend Metro Government pay $10,000 subject to the execution of a release containing broad indemnity language. He added that he would only make that recommendation if Ms. Lang would accept that amount; otherwise, he suggested proceeding to trial.

On March 25, 2025, Bryce Koon responded to Gowen, expressing that there appeared to be a misunderstanding. He stated that he had removed all references to appendix-related issues and treatment, and that the remaining medicals identified in the demand were clearly related to the collision. He asked whether Gowen’s position was that the 9/12/21 ER treatment was not related to the collision, requesting an explanation if so, and offered to resend the records if needed. The message concluded with Koon’s signature and the firm’s confidentiality provisions.

Throughout these exchanges, the parties repeatedly referenced the case details, the amount of medical expenses incurred, and the extent of indemnity language necessary for any proposed settlement, all within the framework of ongoing negotiations seeking a resolution without proceeding to trial.

item_15.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-775]. Client: Brenda Lang. This narrative covers the Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet matter (Jefferson County, 23-CI-005931), focusing on a series of 2025 settlement communications between counsel.

On January 27, 2025, W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, sent a revised settlement demand to Scott Gowen regarding the above-referenced case. In that message, Bryce explained that he had removed all references to appendix-related issues and treatment from the demand. He noted that while there were still questions about the connection of those appendix-related items to the collision, the parties recognized the difficulty of litigating such matters. The removal was presented as a good-faith effort to facilitate a resolution. Bryce stated that even with those items removed, Ms. Lang had incurred more than $26,000 in medical expenses directly related to the injuries caused by his client’s negligence, and that an offer in the range of $5,000 to $10,000 would not be sufficient to resolve the matter. He expressed willingness to discuss a reasonable resolution based on the documented medical expenses and Ms. Lang’s pain and suffering, and he asked Gowen to review the revised demand and supporting materials. The email included a Dropbox link for the supporting documents and carried the standard confidentiality and privilege notices typical of such correspondence. It also contained a caution that the message originated outside Louisville Metro, advising recipients to exercise care with links and attachments, and reiterating that the information was intended only for the named recipient.

On February 17, 2025, Bryce followed up with Gowen, asking for any updates.

On March 5, 2025, Bryce sent another brief note to Gowen, again bridging the communication and reiterating the request for updates, while the message itself carried the customary external-email caution and confidentiality boilerplate.

In a reply dated March 7, 2025, Gowen acknowledged the difficulty in reaching a settlement and stated that he could not accept $35,000. He explained that any settlement would have to include an indemnity for the medicals, noting that many of the medical expenses were not attributable to the accident. Gowen indicated that he did not have authority to settle but would be willing to recommend that Metro Government pay $10,000, subject to the execution of a release containing broad indemnity language. He emphasized that he would make that recommendation only if Ms. Lang would accept the amount; otherwise, he suggested proceeding to trial.

Finally, on March 25, 2025, Bryce responded to Gowen's position, signaling a continued disagreement. He stated that he believed they were not on the same page and explained that he had removed any “questionably-related” treatment concerning Ms. Lang’s appendix. Bryce asked whether Gowen contends that the September 12, 2021 ER treatment was not related to the collision and requested an explanation if that was Gowen’s position. He also offered to resend the records if needed.

Throughout these exchanges, the correspondence reflects the case details (Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet, Jefferson County, 23-CI-005931), the issue of whether certain medical treatments were connected to the collision, the removal of appendix-related treatment from the settlement demand, the claimed medical expenses (over $26,000), and the ongoing negotiation about a potential settlement versus trial. The discussions repeatedly reference the possibility of Metro Government paying a limited amount ($10,000) with broad indemnity language, contingent on Ms. Lang’s willingness to accept, and the alternative of proceeding to trial if a satisfactory resolution could not be reached.

Across these emails, the communications demonstrate a continuing effort to move toward a settlement in Brenda Lang’s MVA case against Marcus Hamlet. The January 27, 2025 message outlined a revised settlement demand, the February 17, 2025 message sought updates, and the March 5, 2025 message issued another follow-up, all seeking resolution in light of the medical expenses Lang incurred and the related injuries. All messages were authored by W. item_47.note: In the case Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-646], involving client Brenda Lang, the client and the defendant were deposed today. The client's deposition went pretty poorly, as she had a lot of trouble explaining how the collision occurred and she changed her story a couple of times; at one point she testified that the vehicle in the damage photos was not the vehicle she collided with.

The Defendant testified that he could not remember anything about the collision—specifically where he was coming from, where he was going to, or what street he was on when the wreck occurred. The police report and the incident reports by the driver and passenger contradict one another.

I spoke to DC and said I would be sending over a demand soon. He candidly told me they would likely make a small offer but are not going to accept the appendectomy as related (two days post-MVA). I told him I would send him over a demand with everything and we can talk numbers in the future.

This document records the sequence of email communications regarding the deposition schedule in Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet. The exchange began in 2024 and involved The Whaley Law Firm, specifically Sarena M. Tuttle, a Litigation Paralegal and Workers’ Compensation Case Manager, and W. Bryce Koon, Esq., along with Scott Gowen, who appears to be counsel for the defendant, Marcus Hamlet. The parties discussed dates for deposing Brenda Lang (the plaintiff) and Marcus Hamlet (the defendant), with hosting arrangements and notices to be issued.

On September 5, 2024, at 3:12 p.m. Eastern Time, Sarena Tuttle wrote to Scott Gowen, with Bryce Koon and Brenda Lang’s filevine email included in copy. In her message she stated, “That sounds perfect. Oct 25, 2024 starting at 9am w/PL immediately followed by Def at your office. We will get a notice over to you as well. Thank you so much.” She signed as Sarena M. Tuttle, Litigation Paralegal & Workers’ Compensation Case Manager at The Whaley Law Firm, located at 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222, with direct line [PHONE-278] and office line [PHONE-279] ext 104, and noted her new email address Sarena@WhaleyLawFirm.com. The message was accompanied by her professional signature.

Earlier on the same day,  Scott Gowen replied at 2:53 p.m., proposing a deposition on Friday, October 25, with Ms. Lang to be deposed at 9:00 a.m. and Bryce Koon to depose Mr. Hamlet immediately thereafter. Gowen stated he was happy to host and asked to be advised whether the date worked so he could issue a notice.

The thread also contains the preceding exchanges, beginning with Gowen’s September 4, 2024, 1:07 p.m. inquiry in which he asked whether September 18 would work, offering to depose Ms. Lang at 9:00 a.m. and have Bryce depose Mr. Hamlet immediately after, noting his willingness to host. In response, on September 4, 2024, at 11:34 a.m., Sarena Tuttle wrote back to Gowen, addressing him as “Mr. Gowen,” explaining that they were circling back on the matter from the prior week and hoping he had enjoyed his trip. She asked him to provide deposition dates as soon as he was settled back in.

Earlier still, on August 28, 2024, at 9:43 a.m., Sarena Tuttle acknowledged Gowen’s update with, “Good morning Mr. Gowen, We completely understand. Thank you for the update.” And on August 27, 2024, at 4:04 p.m., Gowen responded that he was out of town that week and would provide dates the following week. In turn, on August 27, 2024, at 2:39 p.m., Sarena wrote that, as of July 15, 2024, they had requested dates to depose the defendant client and had yet to receive a response, asking Gowen to provide dates when his client would be available for deposition.

The chain shows a prior July 15, 2024 exchange in which Sarena noted the office was fairly open in September and could accommodate most Tuesdays, Wednesdays, or Thursdays after the 10th, requesting a narrowed set of dates from Gowen. The thread also reflects an earlier line of communication from April 2024. On April 9, 2024, Gowen asked Bryce Koon for an extension, requesting until the end of the month to serve responses and apologizing for the oversight and delay. Bryce replied later that same day, at 11:02 a.m., that after reviewing the file it appeared they had not received Gowen’s responses and that they would follow up. Gowen then replied at 3:53 p.m. requesting until the end of the month to serve the responses, to which Bryce responded at 4:04 p.m. with an acknowledgment that it was acceptable (the exact wording in that message reads, “That is okay with me. Thank you for getting back to me.”).

Throughout these communications, multiple messages included the standard outside-sender caution banner indicating that the email originated outside Louisville Metro, advising recipients not to click links or open attachments unless the sender’s address and content were recognized as safe. These cautions appeared at various points in the threaded messages, including the September 4, 2024 and July 27–August 28, 2024 entries.

In sum, the record shows a protracted scheduling dialogue beginning in April 2024 and extending through September 2024, culminating in an agreed proposed deposition plan for October 25, 2024, with Brenda Lang’s deposition at 9:00 a.m., followed by Marcus Hamlet’s deposition immediately afterward, to be hosted at Gowen’s office, and with a notice to be issued to the opposing party. The participants consistently referenced coordinating notices, hosting arrangements, and the availability windows noted in their correspondence, while also noting periods when schedules were open or booked and the need to secure dates after October 1 and specifically targeting October 18, October 25, and other mid- to late-October slots as the case progressed.

Please let me know if this works for you guys and I will get a notice out.” Sarena responded at 3:13 PM the same day, writing, “That sounds perfect. Sarena confirmed the arrangement in a follow-up message on September 6, 2024 at 9:49:04 GMT-04:00, stating, “Thank you so much Mr. Gowen, Our notice is attached as well and will be filed via CourtNet momentarily.” Her signature again listed The Whaley Law Firm’s address, phone numbers, and her new email address.

item_95.note: Case: Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-439]. The file concerns Brenda Lang in the motor vehicle accident case Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet, with Brenda Lang as the client.

In February 2024, a sequence of discovery-related emails unfolded between counsel for Brenda Lang and the City of Louisville’s representatives. On Monday, February 12, 2024, at 8:52 a.m., Scott Gowen sent an email to Aaron Whaley and Bryce Koon, with Scott Gowen cc’d, regarding the Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet matter. He attached the discovery requests for Ms. Lang in both Word and PDF formats and asked the recipients to confirm receipt. The subject line was Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet. The message included a standard confidentiality disclaimer stating that the information is confidential and intended only for the recipient, and warning that any disclosure or action in relation to the contents by others is prohibited.

At 9:18 a.m. on the same day, Monday, February 12, 2024, Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, replied: “Received. We will get these back to you promptly.” His message included his law firm’s contact information and the same attorney‑client privilege disclaimer as part of the signature block.

Later that same day, the February 12 email thread shows additional correspondence from Bryce Koon at 11:02 a.m. in a separate entry, continuing the same discussion about discovery. The request remained that the discovery responses be provided, and the message again carried the standard confidentiality disclaimer.

On Friday, February 12, 2024, at 9:18 a.m. (the earlier February 12 message with Bryce’s reply preceded this timestamp in the record), Scott Gowen also sent a message indicating that he had attached discovery requests in Word and PDF for Ms. Lang to answer and requested confirmation of receipt. The message carried the confidential‑disclaimer language as part of the chain.

Moving to April 2024, on Tuesday, April 9, 2024, at 11:02:17 a.m., Bryce Koon sent another message to Scott Gowen with the subject RE: Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet. In this email, Bryce wrote that after reviewing his file, it did not appear that the client’s discovery responses had been received yet, and he asked when they could be expected. He again included the standard attorney‑client privilege disclaimer in his email signature block.

Later that same day, at 3:53 p.m., Tuesday, April 9, 2024, Scott Gowen replied to Bryce’s earlier message. He asked for until the end of the month to serve the responses and apologized for an oversight and delay. He signed with his name and included the “Get Outlook for iOS” mobile signature line. The message was accompanied by the same confidentiality disclaimer.

Finally, at 4:04 p.m. on Tuesday, April 9, 2024, Bryce Koon responded to Scott Gowen with, “Scott, That is okay with me. Thank you for getting back to me.” His message again included his standard firm signature and the attorney‑client privilege disclaimer.

Across these exchanges, the participants included W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm (address: 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222; phone [PHONE-396]; fax [PHONE-397]) and Scott Gowen (associated with Louisville Metro), along with Sarena Tuttle (cc’d) and the file-specific recipient address [EMAIL-398] (cc’d in the communications). Each email contained the customary confidentiality and privilege notices advising that the message is intended only for the named recipient and that any inadvertent receipt should be reported to the sender, with no waiver of privilege.

item_97.note: Case: Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-427]). Client: Brenda Lang.

In February 2024, discovery was being circulated between counsel. On February 12, 2024, Scott Gowen sent an email to Aaron Whaley and Bryce Koon of The Whaley Law Firm, with Sarena Tuttle copied, attaching discovery requests for Ms. Lang to answer in both Word and PDF formats and asking for confirmation of receipt. The message also carried a confidentiality disclaimer stating that the information was confidential and intended only for the named recipients. Earlier that same morning (8:52 AM), Gowen had sent a similar note requesting receipt confirmation for the Word and PDF discovery documents.

Later that day, at 9:18 AM, Bryce Koon replied: “Received. We will get these back to you promptly,” indicating acknowledgment of receipt. His reply also included the standard attorney‑client confidentiality disclaimer common to the firm’s correspondence.

On April 9, 2024, the discovery process appeared unsettled. At 11:02 AM, Bryce Koon sent an email noting that, after reviewing his file, the firm did not appear to have received the client’s discovery responses yet and asking when they could be expected, expressing appreciation for Scott Gowen’s assistance. Later that same day, at 7:53 PM UTC, Gowen replied: “Bryce, May I please have until the end of this month to serve the responses? I apologize for the oversight and delay.” Bryce’s April 9 message also carried a caution stating that the email came from outside Louisville Metro and advising caution with links or attachments.

item_98.note: In the case Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-425], the client is Brenda Lang, in the matter of Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet, a motor vehicle accident case. The communications summarized below concern discovery, exchanged between The Whaley Law Firm, representing Brenda Lang, and Scott Gowen of Louisville/Jefferson County government.

On February 12, 2024, Scott Gowen sent an email to Aaron Whaley and Bryce Koon with the subject Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet, attaching discovery requests for Ms. Lang to answer in Word and PDF format and asking for confirmation of receipt. The message indicated that Scott had attached discovery requests for Ms. Lang, and he requested that the recipients confirm receipt. The email was cc’d to Scott Gowen and included a confidentiality notice typical of such communications, noting that the information is confidential and privileged and should not be shared with unintended recipients.

Later that same day, February 12, 2024 at 9:18 a.m., Bryce Koon, Esq., responded: “Received. We will get these back to you promptly.” This reply, like the initial message, carried a standard attorney‑client privilege disclaimer and contact information for The Whaley Law Firm.

On April 9, 2024, at 11:02 a.m., Bryce Koon sent an email to Scott Gowen with the subject Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet. In the body, he wrote: “Good morning Scott, After reviewing my file, it does not appear we have received your client’s discovery responses yet. Could you please let us know when we may expect such responses? Your assistance in this matter is greatly appreciated.” The message was cc’d to Sarena Tuttle and to [EMAIL-424]. The signature block identified W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, with the firm’s address at 712 Lyndon Lane, Louisville, KY 40222, and listed the phone number [PHONE-411] and fax number [PHONE-412]. This email also bore the firm’s standard confidentiality disclaimer, indicating that the message is intended only for the named recipient and that any disclosure or forwarding by unintended recipients could be unlawful or a waiver of privilege.

In sum, there was a February 12, 2024 exchange in which discovery requests were forwarded to Brenda Lang’s counsel (and acknowledged as received), followed by a separate April 9, 2024 inquiry from Brenda Lang’s counsel requesting an expected timeline for the opposing party’s discovery responses.

item_100.note: In the case Brenda Lang v. Marcus Hamlet (Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-422]), serving as the plaintiff, Brenda Lang, the discovery correspondence unfolded between February and April 2024. The matter was treated as a motor vehicle accident case (MVA), and the client was Brenda Lang.

On February 12, 2024, at 8:52 a.m., Scott Gowen, counsel for the Louisville Metropolitan Government, sent an email to W. Bryce Koon, Esq., and Aaron Whaley with discovery requests for Ms. Lang to answer. The discovery requests were attached in Word and PDF format. Gowen asked for confirmation of receipt and copied Sarena Tuttle and [EMAIL-423] on the communication. The message included the confidentiality disclaimer typical of such emails.

Later that same day, at 9:18 a.m., Bryce Koon replied. He acknowledged receipt of the discovery requests and stated that they would be returned promptly. His reply included the standard confidentiality notice and caution that the email originated outside Louisville Metro, advising recipients not to click links or forward the content if received in error. Koon's signature contained his firm’s contact details.

Before this February exchange, Gowen’s initial note to the defense team was explicit about attaching discovery requests for Ms. Lang to answer; he asked the recipients to confirm receipt and again included the confidentiality disclaimer in his message.

Several weeks later, on April 9, 2024, additional correspondence occurred. At 11:02 a.m., Bryce Koon wrote that, after reviewing his file, it did not appear that the defense had received the client’s discovery responses yet and asked Scott Gowen to indicate when those responses might be expected. The message carried the outside-mail caution and the standard confidentiality disclaimer, with Koon’s usual contact information in the signature.

That afternoon, at 3:53 p.m., Gowen responded. He requested, in turn, to have until the end of the month to serve the responses and apologized for the oversight and delay.

Less than an hour later, at 4:04 p.m., Koon replied to Gowen with a short, accommodating acknowledgment: “Scott, That is okay with me. Thank you for getting back to me.” This message also carried the standard confidentiality notice.

Throughout these exchanges, the emails consistently included confidentiality language noting attorney-client privilege and instructing that, if received in error, the recipient should contact The Whaley Law Firm and refrain from using or forwarding the information. The communications involved the principal participants—W. Bryce Koon, Esq., of The Whaley Law Firm, representing Brenda Lang, and Scott Gowen, representing the Louisville Metropolitan Government—with Sarena Tuttle cc’d on the messages and [EMAIL-424] also included in the CC field.

item_195.note: In the Brenda Lang motor vehicle accident matter (Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-758]), relating to claim number 410220052, counsel exchanged a series of emails on May 22–23, 2023. The participants included Aaron Whaley of Whaley Law Firm, communicating with Clarke McCulloch of the insured’s representatives, with CC copies to Chris Welch, Jessa Galosmo, Justin Chumbley, and a filevine email for Brenda Lang’s matter.

On May 22, 2023 at 2:59 PM, Aaron Whaley wrote to Clarke McCulloch, with Chris Welch, Jessa Galosmo, Justin Chumbley, and the Brenda Lang Filevine address copied, attaching the subject “410220052.” In the email, Whaley acknowledged receipt of Clarke’s denial and stated that Clarke’s basis was that his client was “confused on what transpired.” Whaley contended that the confusion was about where Clarke’s driver came from, noting that if someone turned right in front of his client, she might be confused about their origin as well. He asked whether Clarke’s vehicle had a camera, suggesting that a camera could potentially resolve the dispute, and requested a response. The message concluded with Whaley’s signature block, including his firm’s contact information: Whaley Law Firm, 712 Lyndon Ln, Louisville, KY 40222, [PHONE-759].

Later that evening, Clarke responded on May 22, 2023 at 6:22 PM. He explained that the incident involved a service truck belonging to the Louisville Metro Police Department (LMPD) and that the vehicle was not equipped with a camera. Despite this, he stated that both the driver and passenger attested they had a green light and that “your client ran the red.” He added that any evidence to the contrary would be taken into consideration. This response was followed by Clarke’s standard title and contact details.

The following day, May 23, 2023, at 2:58 PM, Whaley replied to Clarke (with the same CC recipients and subject line). He argued that a passenger in the vehicle should not be considered a disinterested third party. He framed the question in terms of likelihood, asking Clarke to consider what was more probable: “A person brazenly runs a red light or a person turns right on red (which they are legally allowed to do) and just doesn't see the person coming?”

At 3:28 PM on May 23, 2023, Clarke sent another response reiterating the core issue. He described the matter as “really a very simple matter,” noting that there were two drivers each claiming a green light at an intersection where only one could have had the green. He stated that the other vehicle sustained significant damage and that Whaley’s side had not issued a demand against Ms. Lang because the burden of proof lay with the claimant, and there was no evidence proving that Clarke’s client had the green light. He added that now Whaley represents the claimant, and the claimant bears the same burden of proof, and he posed the question, “How do you prove that we ran the red light?” The email concluded with Clarke’s signature, identifying him as Property & Casualty Claims Manager.

Finally, on May 23, 2023 at 3:31 PM, Whaley wrote back to Clarke (with the same CC list and subject). He stated: “Clarke, testimony is proof. It would be up to the jury who to believe. They could believe based upon your driver and passenger that Ms. Lang ran the light. They could believe Ms. Lang that your insured turned right on red.” This message continued the discussion about how the testimony of the participants could be interpreted by a jury.

The chain also included Clarke’s prior note from May 22, emphasizing that the service truck involved did not have a camera and that the driver and passenger had attested to a green light, as well as Whaley’s earlier assertion that the client was not confused about what happened but rather about where Clarke’s driver came from. The correspondence consistently referenced claim number 410220052 and involved the ongoing strategic dispute over which party had the right-of-way at the intersection and how best to prove who ran the red light.
item_195.note_summary: Lang | In the Brenda Lang motor vehicle... | 2023-05-23

item_195.uuid: ddef3bc2-90e6-4051-94a9-65bcf19840c7

item_196.last_activity: 2023-05-23T00:00:00
item_196.note: This narrative covers Brenda Lang’s motor vehicle accident case ( Brenda-Lang-MVA-[DOB-761]), specifically claim number 410220052, based on correspondence from May 22–23, 2023 between Brenda Lang’s counsel and the insurer’s representative. The underlying accident occurred on September 3, 2021, and involved Lang as the claimant against a Louisville Metro Police Department (LMPD) service truck. The accident diagram referenced in the discussion shows Lang’s vehicle damage at the 1:00 position (passenger front) and the other vehicle’s damage at the 11:00 position (driver front). The police report does not specify who struck whom. The service vehicle in question was described as a Ford F-450, nearly 20 feet long and weighing over four tons.

On May 23, 2023 at 7:00 PM, Aaron Whaley of Whaley Law Firm wrote to Clarke McCulloch, the Property & Casualty Claims Manager for the opposing insurer, noting Lang’s view that the other driver “didn’t get very far into his turn before the impact,” and that the report does not identify who hit whom. Whaley emphasized that Lang’s client had no time to see a 20-foot-long vehicle and stated that he had litigated similar cases before and was prepared to litigate again. He also noted that there is more than one way to approach these cases and that he was comfortable with either path.

Clarke McCulloch later responded, pointing out that the police report indicates Unit #1 (Lang) stated the same about her approach but appeared confused about where Unit #2 (the truck) came from. He highlighted that the truck—a Ford F-450—was nearly 20 feet long and weighed over four tons, and that Lang hit it. He questioned how she could be confused about where a vehicle this large came from unless she wasn’t paying attention, and he suggested that missing the color of the light could also be easy in this scenario. He described the situation as “most likely,” but admitted he could not prove it and would need proof to support a claim. He said they were willing to review any evidence establishing the light color but would not agree to pay a claim when the only participants have opposing accounts and the only witnesses are two non-disinterested third parties.

In a subsequent message, Whaley argued that testimony is proof and that a jury would decide whom to believe. He stated that the jury could credit either the driver and passenger who said Lang ran the light or Lang’s version that the insured turned right on red.

Earlier in the thread, Clarke had stated that this was a simple matter: two drivers, both claiming they had a green light at an intersection where only one could have had the green. The insurer’s vehicle suffered significant damage, but no demand had been issued against Lang because the burden of proof lies with the claimant. Now that counsel represents the claimant, the same burden applies, and the question remains how to prove that the other party ran the red light.

Clarke had previously informed Whaley that the service truck belonged to LMPD and was not equipped with a camera, but that both the driver and passenger attested that they had the green light and that Lang ran it. He added that any evidence to the contrary would be considered.

Whaley responded by noting that he did not regard a passenger in Lang’s vehicle as a disinterested third party. He asked for Clarke’s view on what is more likely: a person brazenly running a red light or a person turning right on red and simply failing to see the other vehicle. He also asked whether Clarke’s vehicle had a camera, suggesting that camera evidence could resolve the dispute, and asked Clarke to let him know.

Across the exchanges, the parties repeatedly referenced the absence of disinterested third-party witnesses, the lack of camera evidence on the LMPD service truck, and the competing accounts of the accident. The communications reflect ongoing efforts to determine liability and the potential value of light-color evidence, with both sides signaling willingness to review new evidence but remaining cautious about accepting liability without additional proof.

item_197.note: Case: Brenda Lang MVA-[DOB-763]. The client is Brenda Lang, and the file reference for the motor vehicle accident is 410220052.

Between May 22 and May 23, 2023, Brenda Lang’s counsel, Aaron Whaley of Whaley Law Firm, engaged in a sequence of email exchanges with Clarke McCulloch, Property & Casualty Claims Manager. The May 22, 2023 correspondence began with Aaron acknowledging receipt of a denial on the claim and challenging the basis for denial. He stated that the basis claimed—that his client was “confused on what transpired”—was inaccurate, explaining that Brenda was not confused about what happened, but rather about where the service driver came from since the driver turned in front of her. He asked whether the vehicle had a camera, suggesting that such footage could possibly resolve the dispute, and asked for a reply.

In Clarke McCulloch’s May 22, 2023 reply, he indicated that the incident involved a service truck belonging to the Louisville Metro Police Department (LMPD). He noted the truck was not equipped with a camera. He stated that both the driver and passenger attested that they had a green light and that Brenda Lang ran the red. He added that any evidence to the contrary would be taken into consideration.

On May 23, 2023, at 2:58 PM, Aaron sent another message to Clarke. He wrote: “Clarke, I do not consider a passenger in the vehicle to be a disinterested third party. As a practical matter trying hard not to be partisan, what do you think is more likely? A person brazenly runs a red light or a person turns right on red (which they are legally allowed to do) and just doesn't see the person coming?” This message was part of continuing discussion about liability and the available evidence.

The May 23 message also includes the earlier May 22 correspondence from Aaron, which reiterated his questions and concerns and requested information about whether the service vehicle had a camera to help resolve the dispute. The email chain shows Aaron Whaley’s law firm details (Whaley Law Firm, 712 Lyndon Ln, Louisville, KY 40222, [PHONE-764]) and the standard law firm signature, along with the partial contact list and file reference 410220052.

In summary, the exchange documented that the case centers on a collision involving a service truck from LMPD, which reportedly had no camera, with both the driver and passenger claiming they had a green light and Brenda Lang running a red; Brenda’s counsel sought to understand the opposing view, questioned the potential influence of a passenger as a non-disinterested party, and repeatedly asked whether a camera existed to resolve the dispute.
